# Author's Note

Before you begin, I want to be clear about who I am and what this book is.

## The Reach

I grew up as a competitive athlete. I spent years fascinated by how my body worked, why certain training produced results, how movement translated to performance, what separated good from elite. As a high schooler choosing a path for college, I faced a genuine dilemma: should I study the human body through biology (anatomy, physiology, kinesiology) or through mechanics and engineering?

I had no one in my network who understood both worlds. So, I did something that felt desperate at the time: I cold-emailed an astronaut.

My logic was simple. Astronauts are elite physical performers who also often happen to be engineers. They've had to understand the human body from both angles; their lives depend on it. I found one online, wrote a sincere email explaining my situation, and asked for advice.

He responded. His guidance was direct: go into engineering. If you become an engineer, you can always learn the anatomy and physiology later. It's much harder to go the other direction.

That single email changed the course of my life. I share this because the same principle applies to what follows in this book: sometimes the path forward requires reaching out before you feel qualified, asking questions before you have standing, and acting before you're certain.

## The Wager

In the Prologue, I lay out the wager that frames everything in this book. The short version: the asymmetry of outcomes makes this bet clear. Preparing for AI transformation has enormous upside and modest downside. Ignoring it has small upside and potentially catastrophic downside. Only one side of this wager protects your future.

## A Guide, Not a Guru

I am a guide, not a guru. A fellow journeyman, not an oracle.

I'm not writing this book from some elevated position where I've figured it all out. I'm writing it from the trenches, as someone who has been building with AI since before the current hype cycle began, and who has watched the same leadership challenges repeat across several technology transitions.

In 2015, I was leading a team that was integrating IBM Watson into physical products before most people knew what a large language model was. In 2022, I was among the first users building with enterprise AI tools when PwC partnered with OpenAI. The technology changed dramatically between those moments. The human challenges didn't.

This book is not a proven playbook. No one has that. The AI transformation is too new, too varied, and too fast-moving for anyone to claim definitive answers. Anyone who tells you otherwise is selling certainty they don't possess.

What this book offers is a reasoned bet: a synthesis of principles that have worked in every technology transition I've observed, applied to the current moment. These frameworks are grounded in decades of research on systems thinking, organizational learning, and decision-making under uncertainty. They draw on thinkers like Deming, Senge, Kahneman, and Taleb, people whose ideas have proven their worth across previous waves of technological change.

I'm confident in what I've experienced. I'm confident in the patterns I've observed. And I wanted to share with the world what I'm seeing as an early adopter who is building alongside you.

But I'm not pretending to know it all. No one does. We're all just trying to figure it out. Let's go on the journey together.

## How to Read This Book

This book is organized around five pillars: Mindset, Culture, Systems, Orchestration, and Recursion. Each pillar builds on the previous, and together they form a complete operating model.

You can read straight through from beginning to end. The book is designed for that. Each chapter references what came before and sets up what comes next.

But you can also dip in based on your current needs:

If you're struggling with strategic clarity, start with Part I (Mindset).

If you're struggling with team adoption, start with Part II (Culture).

If you're struggling with reliable execution, start with Part III (Systems).

If you're struggling with coordination, start with Part IV (Orchestration).

If you're struggling with continuous improvement, start with Part V (Recursion).

Each chapter can stand alone, but the real power comes from understanding how the pieces connect.

## On Frameworks

This book introduces many frameworks, the Value Flow Engine, the 3L Ladder, the Two-Mode Decision System, the Building Block System Design, and others.

These frameworks are tools, not rules. They're designed to help you think, not to constrain you.

Use what's useful. Adapt what needs adapting. Discard what doesn't fit your context. The goal isn't to implement every framework, it's to develop the judgment to know which tools serve which situations.

## On Technology

I've intentionally avoided tying this book to specific tools, platforms, or vendors.

Technology changes fast. By the time you read this, some of the AI tools available today may be obsolete, and new capabilities will have emerged. Any book that depends on specific technology will quickly become dated.

Instead, I've focused on principles that transcend specific tools. The frameworks in this book are designed to be evergreen, applicable whether you're using today's AI or whatever comes next.

When I use examples, they're illustrative, not prescriptive. The specific tools matter less than the patterns of thinking.

## On Certainty and Humility

The AI field is moving rapidly. What we believe today may be contradicted tomorrow. Best practices are evolving. Edge cases are still being discovered.

Throughout this book, I share frameworks and recommendations based on patterns I've observed and principles that have proven durable across technology transitions. But I hold these with appropriate humility. I've been wrong before. Some of what I've written will likely prove incomplete as the field evolves.

What I'm confident about are the evergreen principles: the value of clarity, the importance of culture, the need for systems thinking, the power of learning. These have transcended every previous technology wave. I believe they'll transcend this one too.

The specific tactics may evolve. The principles endure.

## On Practice

Reading this book won't make you AI-ready. Practice will.

Each chapter includes practical applications. Each framework includes steps for implementation. But the real learning happens when you try these approaches in your own context, with your own challenges.

My recommendation: don't wait until you've finished the book to start applying. Pick something from Part I and try it this week. Let your experience inform your reading. Let your reading inform your next experiment.

The flywheel I describe in this book works for individuals too. Try, learn, adjust, repeat. That's how capability develops.

## On Feedback

This book is version 1.0.

Like any system worth building, it will improve through feedback. If you find errors, gaps, or opportunities for improvement, I want to know. If you apply these frameworks and discover something valuable, I want to hear about it.

The community of practitioners who engage with these ideas will make them better. I'm committed to incorporating what we learn together into future versions.

## Gratitude

Writing this book was a team effort, even though only one name appears on the cover.

I'm grateful to the leaders who shared their experiences with me, the struggles, the breakthroughs, the lessons learned the hard way. I'm grateful to the colleagues who challenged my thinking and improved my frameworks. I'm grateful to my family for their patience during the many hours of writing and revision.

And I'm grateful to you for reading. The ideas in this book only matter if they help people lead more effectively. By engaging with these ideas, you're giving them life.

Let's build something worth building.

Onward, Ben S. Cooper

January 2026

<!-- PAGE BREAK -->

# Prologue: The Only Bet That Makes Sense

*"We are kept from our goal not by obstacles but by a clear path to a lesser goal."*

— Robert Brault

It was 2015. I stood in my lab at VF Corporation's innovation center, holding a jacket that shouldn't exist.

I wirelessly connected it to a laptop on our workbench, and the garment was doing something no piece of clothing had ever done: responding to my body. Sensors in the fabric monitored the environment inside the clothes. IBM Watson processed that data along with weather forecasts. Heating elements in the shell warmed up on their own when the system detected I was getting cold.

Servers hummed. Cables snaked across workbenches cluttered with fabric samples and circuit boards. I was leading materials scientists, electrical engineers, software developers, and apparel designers, groups that had never worked together this way. The jacket worked. It actually worked.

Fast forward eight years. I'm at PwC, and the company partners with OpenAI to develop ChatPwC. Different technology. Different company. Different decade. But again, I found myself at the frontier, one of the first people building with a tool that would change everything.

PwC made a choice: embrace AI as a force multiplier, not fight it. I watched what happens when an organization moves early and moves with purpose. They learn faster. They stack advantages. They lead instead of follow.

That experience, almost two decades at the frontier, seeing what works when leaders get it right, is why I wrote this book.

## The Wager

You are standing at a decision point.

Not a crossroads with clear signs. Not a fork with well-worn paths. This is more basic: a wager about the future itself.

Here's the bet: Either AI will reshape how organizations create value, make decisions, and compete, or it won't.

If you bet that AI will change everything and you're right, you'll be prepared. You'll have built the skills, the mental models, and the organizational muscle to thrive. You'll lead through the change, not scramble to catch up.

If you bet that AI will change everything and you're wrong? You still win. You'll have invested in clear thinking, fast learning, and the ability to adapt. These serve any organization, with or without AI.

Now consider the other side.

If you bet that AI won't reshape the landscape and you're right, you save some effort. Business as usual.

But if you bet against change and you're wrong, the cost isn't just missing an opportunity. You find yourself running a twentieth-century operation in a twenty-first-century world. Competitors move faster. Your best people leave for organizations that invest in the future. Customers expect things you can't deliver. The cost isn't inconvenience. It's irrelevance.

This is Pascal's Wager for the age of AI.

The philosopher Blaise Pascal argued that when facing uncertainty about God's existence, the smart choice is to live as if God exists. Why? Because the upside of belief is infinite, while the downside is small. You might be wrong, but you'd still have lived a good life. The imbalance of outcomes makes the choice clear.

We face the same kind of imbalance today.

The upside of preparing for AI: competitive advantage, organizational resilience, the ability to attract people who want to work at the frontier. The downside: some investment in learning, a few experiments that don't work out, time building skills that help you regardless.

The upside of ignoring AI: saved effort, comfort, avoiding change. The downside, if AI matters as much as the evidence suggests: strategic irrelevance, talent leaving, competitive collapse.

The math is clear. Only one side of this wager protects your future.

## This Isn't About Hype

Let me be direct: this book is not arguing for hype.

I've spent enough time in innovation labs, venture studios, and strategy teams to be deeply skeptical of technology hype cycles. I've watched bold claims fade. I've seen "transformational initiatives" become PowerPoint graveyards. I know the gap between what vendors promise and what organizations actually achieve.

AI has more than its share of hype. The breathless announcements, the inflated expectations, the solutions looking for problems: they're real. Be skeptical.

But skepticism of hype is not the same as dismissing the capability.

What I've seen over the past several years, building with these tools, advising organizations through adoption, watching early movers stack advantages, has convinced me that beneath the hype is something real. The technology is improving fast. Applications are multiplying. Organizations that figure this out are pulling ahead in ways that will be hard to catch.

This book isn't asking you to believe in magic. It's asking you to think clearly about uneven bets.

## The Cost of Delay

I see leaders fall into a particular trap: productive hesitation.

It looks like diligence. It sounds responsible. "We're evaluating options." "We're waiting for the technology to mature." "We're building our AI strategy." "We need more data before we commit."

In past technology cycles, this caution often paid off. You could let early adopters work out the bugs, then move when the path was clearer. The cost of waiting was manageable. The risk of moving too early was real.

But this moment is different.

Organizations learning now aren't just building skills. They're building the muscle memory of adaptation itself. They're developing knowledge about what works, what doesn't, and how to improve. That learning compounds. Every experiment creates data that shapes the next one.

Meanwhile, organizations that wait aren't standing still. They're falling behind because the frontier keeps moving. By the time they decide to act, the gap they need to close is bigger than when they started waiting.

This is the paradox of delay: caution meant to reduce risk often increases it. What feels like smart patience is often slow retreat.

## What This Book Offers

I wrote this book for leaders who sense something big is happening and want more than tactics.

Not a list of AI tools to try. Not a hype-filled vision of automated utopia. Not a fear-driven warning about machines taking your job. And not a guru claiming all the answers.

Instead, this book offers an operating model: a systematic way to build organizations that can thrive in an age of intelligent machines. It's built on a simple idea: the technology matters less than how you lead.

I'm offering this as a fellow traveler, not an oracle. I've been building with AI for over a decade, and I've noticed patterns that keep repeating. The frameworks in this book are my summary of those patterns, grounded in principles that have held up across every technology shift I've seen.

AI doesn't replace leadership. It amplifies it. If your thinking is clear, AI speeds up clarity. If your organization learns, AI compounds that learning. If your culture shares knowledge openly, AI has more to work with. If your systems are well-designed, AI makes them stronger.

The organizations that will win aren't those that adopt AI fastest. They're those that build the foundations, in mindset, culture, systems, coordination, and learning, that let them adapt to whatever comes next.

That's what this book teaches you to build.

## The Wager, Revisited

Let me return to where we started.

You are standing at a decision point. The evidence suggests AI will reshape how organizations operate, compete, and create value. The evidence doesn't tell you exactly how, or exactly when, or exactly which tools will matter most.

In the face of that uncertainty, you have a choice.

You can wait for clarity. You can hedge. You can hand the question to a task force and hope it resolves itself. You can tell yourself your industry is different, your organization is special, your timeline is longer.

Or you can make the bet that the evidence suggests is smart.

Bet that change is coming. Bet that organizations who prepare will have advantages over those who don't. Bet that the skills you build, clear thinking, fast learning, adaptive systems, will serve you whether the future looks like current predictions or something entirely different.

Bet on preparation. Bet on capability. Bet on yourself and your organization's ability to learn and adapt.

This is the only wager that makes sense.

This book is the path to making that wager pay off.

Let's begin.

**Start Today:**

1. Ask yourself: Which side of the wager am I on? Have I acted on it?

2. Identify one area where you're "productively hesitating," waiting for clarity that won't come

3. Take one small step toward AI readiness today. The math is clear.

# Introduction: The AI-Ready Operating Model

*"We are what we repeatedly do. Excellence, then, is not an act, but a habit."*

— Aristotle (via Will Durant)

It was December 2022, a few weeks after ChatGPT launched, and I was sitting alone in my home office running experiments.

I dropped in the text from an article I'd been interested in summarizing. Within seconds, it came back with a synthesis that would have taken me an hour to write. I asked it to change the tone. It did. I asked it to restructure the argument. It did that too. I pushed harder: "Turn the key points into a framework." It produced something more than half decent.

Those early experiences felt different. The responses seemed personal, almost alive. Was it perfect? Absolutely not. It lost context, got facts wrong, produced occasional nonsense. But the potential was unmistakable. The technology had finally moved beyond hope, genuinely bridging the gap between what humans experience as authentic interaction and what a machine could deliver.

That night, I couldn't sleep. I kept thinking about what this meant for everyone that uses a computer and keyboard to do work, for every organization I advised, for every process I'd spent years helping companies optimize. It was early, but I could feel that the ground was shifting beneath all of it.

You may have had a moment like this. Or you may still be waiting for one.

The question I hear most from leaders is about direction: "We know we need to do something with AI. But we don't know where to start. We don't know what matters most. And honestly, we're not sure we understand what's possible."

That last part deserves attention.

Ilya Sutskever, co-founder of OpenAI and one of the people who built modern AI, put it this way: "The gap between what the best models can do and what most people think they can do is still enormous." [CITATION NEEDED: Sutskever interview/publication source and date]

This gap comes down to exposure. Most leaders haven't seen what's truly possible. Not because they lack curiosity or skill, but because they're running organizations while the frontier moves. What AI can do today, right now, can already change how knowledge workers operate, how decisions get made, how organizations learn. Most people just haven't experienced it yet.

This book exists to close that gap. Not through theory, but through practice. Everything you need to lead in this era already exists. The frameworks, the tools, the patterns are here. What's been missing is a clear path to capability.

That's what this book provides.

The leaders I work with aren't timid. They run companies, divisions, and teams. They've handled disruption before. But this moment feels different: faster, higher stakes, harder to grasp. The technology shifts weekly. The hype is loud. The pressure to act is constant.

After years of building, advising, and transforming organizations in the age of intelligent machines, I've learned this: the technology matters less than you think. What matters is how you lead.

AI doesn't replace leadership. It amplifies it. If your thinking is clear, AI speeds up clarity. If your thinking is muddled, AI speeds up confusion. If your organization learns, AI compounds that learning. If your organization hoards knowledge and resists change, AI will expose every crack in the foundation.

This book is about building an organization, and a leadership practice, that can thrive no matter which tools dominate tomorrow.

## The Operating Model Gap

Most organizations treat AI as a technology project. They form task forces. They run pilots. They hire specialists. And too often, they stall, caught between the hype of possibility and the reality of getting things done.

The problem is they lack a clear operating model.

Your operating model is how your organization actually works. It's the habits, structures, and feedback loops that turn plans into results.

Strategy can change fast when technology shifts. But your operating model takes longer to rebuild. Right now, most organizations are running new technology on old ways of working.

Look at the typical enterprise:

Knowledge lives in silos, guarded by teams

Decisions flow through hierarchy, not evidence

Documentation feels like overhead, not infrastructure

Learning stays personal, not organizational

Speed matters, but quality controls are weak

AI amplifies every one of these patterns. In an organization with poor knowledge sharing, AI tools create islands of skill that never spread. In an organization that values rank over evidence, AI becomes a political tool instead of a source of truth. In an organization without documentation habits, AI has nothing to learn from.

The leaders who win in this era won't be those who adopt AI fastest. They'll be those who build organizations that learn, adapt, and get smarter over time, with or without any specific tool.

There's another gap that's less obvious: the gap between what leaders assume is possible and what's actually doable today. The baseline of what you can do, in how much time, at what cost, keeps shifting. Leaders who anchor to yesterday's limits make decisions with outdated mental models. Part I of this book tackles this directly: how to develop a mindset that stays current, and why communication becomes your main lever when execution barriers collapse.

That's what this book teaches you to build.

## Why AI Is Different: The General Purpose Technology Shift

AI is not just another tech upgrade. It's what economists call a General Purpose Technology (GPT), a category that includes only a handful of shifts in human history: the printing press, the steam engine, electricity, and computing. Economists Timothy Bresnahan and Manuel Trajtenberg defined GPTs by three traits: they apply across many industries, they keep improving, and they spark new inventions.[^gpt]

Throughout history, mastering GPTs has determined who wins and who loses. But here's what matters for you: GPT transitions don't just help incumbents. They move power around.

The printing press didn't just help monasteries copy manuscripts faster. It let new players (individual printers, pamphleteers, reformers) reshape how information flowed. Electricity didn't just power bigger factories. It enabled distributed manufacturing, individual machines, and organizational designs impossible under steam. The telegraph didn't just help existing firms talk faster. It enabled new forms of coordination that gave rise to the modern corporation.

AI follows this pattern. Yes, big organizations are investing heavily. But the deeper shift is this: AI lets a two-person team run systems that used to require hundreds. It lets an individual leader build and scale what once demanded enterprise resources. The scaffolding that used to require massive capital (research, analysis, content creation, workflow automation, customer engagement) can now be assembled by anyone who understands the principles.

This is why the frameworks in this book matter for you, whether you're building from scratch, leading a small team, or transforming a large organization. The core principles are the same. The difference is you can now apply them faster, with fewer resources, at scales once reserved for the well-funded.

The future of value creation will depend less on incumbents than at any time in history. If you stay focused on your value flow engine, if you build the cultural and system foundations, if you master coordination and continuous learning loops, the sky is truly the limit.

## Agency: The Foundation You Must Not Give Away

Before we go further, I need to stake a claim.

Agency is your ability to make things happen. It's not a metaphor. It's the core quality that separates people who shape outcomes from people who just react to them.

Everything in this book is designed to grow your agency, not replace it.

### Agency exists on a spectrum

Agency isn't all-or-nothing. It's a spectrum.

Some people show high agency: they start things, adapt, and shape results. They decide what needs doing, see consequences coming, regulate their own behavior, and reflect on what's working. Others show lower agency: they react, follow, and defer, waiting for direction, accepting defaults, avoiding the discomfort of choosing.

Psychologist Albert Bandura identified four core traits of human agency:[^agency]

Intentionality: forming plans and strategies

Forethought: anticipating outcomes and setting goals

Self-reactiveness: regulating your own behavior

Self-reflectiveness: examining how well you're doing

These traits aren't fixed. They can be built up or allowed to fade.

### AI agents exist on the same spectrum

The word "agent" appears everywhere in AI discussions. We talk about AI agents, autonomous agents, agentic workflows. The language isn't accidental.

An agent, in its simplest sense, is something that acts: it pursues goals, makes decisions, and produces effects. When we build AI agents, we create systems that can act. Some are simple tools that follow commands. Others are developing real agency: the ability to pursue goals, adapt to obstacles, and take actions without being told exactly what to do.

AI agents exist on a spectrum too. And they're evolving toward greater agency with each generation. Today's AI can follow instructions. Some of the most advanced AI systems are setting up and deploying their own sub-goals, changing strategies mid-task, reflecting on its own performance, and attacking projects with a focus and persistence that put even the most diligent team member to shame.

This is just the tip of the iceberg.

### The Question Is About Degrees of Agency

This book takes one question seriously: What happens to human agency when AI agents can do more and more?

Some say it doesn't matter. If AI can do a task, let it. Efficiency wins. The human role becomes oversight at best, irrelevance at worst.

I see it differently.

The question isn't whether humans will "beat" machines. That framing misses the point. The real question is: how much agency do you bring to the work?

When you operate with high agency (setting direction, anticipating results, adapting to feedback, shaping outcomes), you stay the architect. When you give up agency, you become a passenger in systems you no longer direct.

This isn't a competition between humans and machines. It's about who shapes the outcome. In any collaboration, the party with higher agency shapes what happens. The party with lower agency follows.

### High agency is the path to staying relevant

My thesis is simple: for humans to stay relevant as AI grows more capable, we must develop toward high agency.

Agency may be the only thing we can truly hold onto. The ability to decide what should be done, to define what success looks like, to adapt when reality surprises us. This is the essence of leadership.

Consider what happens when leaders give up agency:

They hand off what to build without defining why it matters

They accept AI outputs without checking if they match intent

They chase speed without questioning direction

They lose the ability to course-correct because they no longer understand the course

Take for example, a VP of operations at a mid-sized logistics company outsourced their AI strategy entirely to consultants. The consultants built impressive dashboards and automated reporting workflows. Six months later, when the market shifted and the company needed to pivot, the VP couldn't adjust the systems. He didn't understand what they measured or why. The consultants had moved on to other clients. The AI tools that were supposed to create advantage had created dependency instead.

Contrast that with a founder I advised who took a different path. She spent her first month with AI tools doing the work herself, learning what they could and couldn't do. She built her own prompts, tested her own workflows, documented what worked. When she eventually delegated to her team, she could coach them. When the tools evolved, she could evaluate the changes. She stayed the architect.

The difference wasn't technical skill. It was agency.

Now consider what happens when leaders build high agency:

They design the context that shapes AI work

They define the constraints and success criteria

They build loops that surface when reality drifts from intent

They stay the architects of outcomes, even when they're not the ones executing

Every framework in this book, from context architecture to decision velocity to orchestration to recursion, is an expression of agency. They're tools for leaders to shape outcomes with intent, at scale, with AI as amplifier rather than replacement.

### A philosophical stance

This is a philosophical stance, not a technical one.

I believe preserving and developing human agency is essential to preserving human value. In a future we can't fully predict, where AI will keep growing in ways we can't foresee, the ability to act with purpose, to be the author of direction rather than just the reviewer of outputs, may be the core of what it means to lead.

Some will argue this doesn't matter. I disagree.

The operating model that follows is built on this foundation.

## What This Book Will Give You

This book is organized around five connected capabilities, which I call the five pillars of AI readiness. Each Part goes deep on one pillar.

Part I: Mindset, The Leader's Operating Lens (Chapters 1-4)

How to see your business as a value engine, build AI literacy into advantage, make decisions at speed without recklessness, and prototype for clarity.

Part II: Culture, From Mindset to Movement (Chapters 5-7)

How to scale clarity through shared behavior, build documentation as infrastructure, and replace hierarchy with evidence.

Part III: Systems, From Culture to Consistency (Chapters 8-10)

How to architect your business as a Value Flow Engine, build composable AI systems, and design Human Checkpoints.

Part IV: Orchestration, From Consistency to Flow (Chapters 11-13)

How to design blended workforces of humans and AI agents, sync them through rhythm design, and govern motion through a Control Plane.

Part V: Recursion, From Flow to Evolution (Chapters 14-15)

How to build continuous learning loops and sustain change over time.

Each chapter follows a consistent structure:

An Opening: A problem, scenario, or question to frame the topic

Context and Stakes: Why this matters and what's at risk

Framework or Tool: The core concept or method

Application: How to use it in practice

Transition: Connection to what comes next

Throughout, you'll find practical frameworks, diagnostic tools, and concrete applications. Let these serve as templates in helping you build your own AI-ready operating manual.

Now let me show you how the five pillars work together.

## The AI-Ready Operating Model

The five pillars connect in a specific sequence:

Mindset → Culture → Systems → Orchestration → Recursion

Each pillar builds on the one before it. Each compounds the others. Together, they form a repeating feedback loop: an operating system that gets stronger with every cycle.

[GRAPHIC: framework_intro_leadership-recursion-loop_v1.png]

Caption: The Leadership Recursion Loop, five pillars that form an infinite learning cycle

Let me introduce each pillar.

### Pillar 1: Mindset, The Leader's Operating Lens

Everything begins with how you see.

Mindset is the cognitive foundation: the mental models, thinking habits, and decision patterns that shape how you assess reality and take action.

An AI-ready mindset means:

Seeing your business as a value flow engine, not a collection of departments

Thinking in inputs, nodes, and outcomes, making every effort visible

Making decisions with speed and quality: fast when reversible, careful when permanent

Prototyping to create clarity, not waiting for perfect plans

The AI-ready leader doesn't ask, "What can I automate?" They ask, "Where is value leaking in my engine, and what can AI amplify?"

This pillar introduces the Value Flow Engine as your core business model, the 3L Ladder (Literacy → Leverage → Leadership) as your growth path, and the Two-Mode Decision System for matching decision mode to reversibility.

Part I of this book covers Mindset across four chapters.

### Pillar 2: Culture, From Mindset to Movement

If Mindset answers "How do I think?", Culture answers "How do we work together?"

Culture is the scaling mechanism. It turns individual clarity into team motion by standardizing how people learn, share, decide, and improve.

An AI-ready culture means:

Psychological safety for trying things: people can test, fail, and learn without fear

Openness by default: knowledge flows freely, not through gatekeepers

Shared language: work described as systems, not opinions

Documentation as infrastructure: every artifact becomes a reusable learning unit

Evidence over rank: proof wins over position

Culture determines whether AI skills stay isolated in individuals or become a force that compounds across the organization. Strong culture turns every experiment into organizational learning. Weak culture turns every tool into another silo.

This pillar introduces the Composable Learning Model: the formula that turns documentation into growing intelligence (Documentation × Transparency × Reuse Velocity = Compounding Intelligence).

Part II of this book covers Culture across three chapters.

### Pillar 3: Systems, From Culture to Consistency

Culture creates alignment. Systems create reliability.

Systems are the structural layer that turns values and intent into working reality. They're the processes, tools, interfaces, and governance that produce consistent outcomes even as complexity grows.

An AI-ready systems architecture means:

Building Block System Design: workflows are modular pieces, not custom scripts

Antifragile infrastructure: systems that get stronger through stress, not despite it

Human Checkpoints by design: human judgment is a control surface, not a fallback

Outcome-driven architecture: start from customer value and work backward

Continuous measurement: systems watch themselves and flag drift

This pillar goes deep on the Value Flow Engine as your business physics model: the system-level view of how unmet customer needs convert into delivered value, loyalty, reputation, and value surplus. It also introduces Building Block System Design (your execution architecture) and Human Checkpoints (Review → Approval → Escalation) for calibrating human involvement.

Part III of this book covers Systems across three chapters.

### Pillar 4: Orchestration, From Consistency to Flow

Systems create consistency. Orchestration turns consistency into coordinated motion.

Orchestration is the coordination layer that syncs humans, workflows, and AI agents so the organization moves as one system rather than many competing parts.

An AI-ready orchestration practice means:

Rhythm alignment: humans, systems, and agents operate on synced but distinct rhythms

Direction and magnitude: decisions measured by both strategic fit and impact

Clear handoffs: every transition has criteria and accountability

Flow governance: every workflow has ownership, service-level agreements (SLAs), and escalation paths

Distributed trust: work moves safely between humans and AI through verified checkpoints

Orchestration is not automation. It's coherence in motion. The goal isn't uniform speed; it's intentional timing, like a heart where different chambers beat at different rhythms yet stay perfectly in sync.

This pillar introduces the Control Plane as your governance infrastructure and explores how to design for speed differences between human judgment (slow but contextual) and machine execution (fast but bounded).

Part IV of this book covers Orchestration across three chapters.

### Pillar 5: Recursion, The Continuous Learning Loop

Orchestration creates velocity. Recursion turns velocity into healthy growth.

Recursion is the continuous learning loop that converts action into insight, then converts insight into better behavior, architecture, and decisions.

An AI-ready recursion practice means:

Feedback as fuel: every action generates data that shapes the next cycle

Evidence-based evolution: decisions updated based on real-world results

Antifragility by design: volatility strengthens the system through learning

Learning velocity: shorter time from problem spotted to fix applied

Control Plane intelligence: telemetry becomes the organization's nervous system

This pillar introduces the Recursive Learning Flywheel (Observe → Detect → Interpret → Refactor → Reflect) and the Recursion Console: the tool for turning raw data into action.

And this is the key: Recursion feeds back into Mindset. Every learning cycle produces clarity that informs how leaders think. The loop is infinite: Mindset → Culture → Systems → Orchestration → Recursion → Mindset.

Part V of this book covers Recursion across two chapters.

## How the Pillars Compound

The power of this operating model isn't in any single pillar. It's in how they reinforce each other.

Each turn through the loop strengthens the next. Belief becomes behavior. Behavior becomes structure. Structure becomes coordination. Coordination becomes learning. Learning becomes renewed belief.

This is not a one-time change. It's an ongoing practice: a rhythm of continuous improvement where every cycle builds organizational capability.

**Key Takeaway:** The AI-Ready Operating Model is not a checklist to finish. It's a flywheel to spin. The goal isn't to "complete" transformation; it's to build an organization that gets stronger with every turn.

## Who This Book Is For

This book is written for anyone who wants to lead in the AI era, regardless of title, organization size, or current resources.

You might be:

A solo founder building a company with AI as your first team member

A two-person team scaling operations that would have needed dozens before

An entrepreneur turning an idea into a business at speeds previously impossible

A team leader bringing AI capabilities to your group

A senior executive rethinking how your organization creates value

A consultant helping others navigate change

The principles in these pages apply whether you're starting fresh or transforming something that exists. The frameworks work whether you lead one person or one thousand. The architectures scale up, and perhaps more importantly, they scale down.

I've avoided writing a book just for "enterprises" or "C-suites." Those books exist. What I've seen is more interesting: individual leaders, often with minimal resources, using AI to build and scale what previously demanded massive organizations. Two-person teams running hundred-million-dollar businesses. Solo operators managing complexity that would have required entire departments.

This is the new reality. And it needs a new operating model.

You don't need to be technical. You don't need to understand machine learning algorithms or prompt engineering tricks. What you need is a framework for thinking clearly, building capable systems, and making good decisions under uncertainty, whether you're the only person in the room or leading thousands.

That's what this book provides.

## A Word on Technology and Timelessness

AI moves fast. By the time you read this, some tools I mention may be outdated. New capabilities will have emerged. The landscape will have shifted.

That's intentional.

This book focuses on building organizational capability that outlasts specific tools. The frameworks here are designed to be evergreen: principles that stay true whether you're using today's technology or whatever comes next.

I call this "technology-agnostic AI readiness." The technology will keep moving. Your job is to build an organization that moves with it, learning faster than the rate of change.

## One Step at a Time

Transformation can feel overwhelming. Five pillars might seem like a lot to build.

But here's what I want you to remember: progress beats perfection.

You don't need to transform everything at once. You don't need to master every framework before acting. Start where you are. Pick one workflow. One decision. One practice.

The leadership learning loop is powerful because it compounds. Each small improvement strengthens the foundation for the next. Each learning cycle builds organizational muscle.

The future doesn't belong to organizations that move fastest. It belongs to those that learn fastest: organizations that turn every experiment into insight, every insight into action, and every action into capability.

Start small. Learn visibly. Lead through rhythm.

Let's begin.

Part I begins with the foundation of everything: how you see your business.

[^gpt]: Timothy F. Bresnahan and Manuel Trajtenberg, "General Purpose Technologies: 'Engines of Growth'?" Journal of Econometrics 65, no. 1 (1995): 83-108.

[^agency]: Albert Bandura, "Toward a Psychology of Human Agency," Perspectives on Psychological Science 1, no. 2 (2006): 164-180. Bandura's social cognitive theory identifies intentionality, forethought, self-reactiveness, and self-reflectiveness as the four core properties of human agency.

# PART I: MINDSET — THE LEADER'S OPERATING LENS

<!-- PAGE BREAK -->

# Chapter 1: Seeing Business as a Value Flow Engine

*"A bad system will beat a good person every time."*

— W. Edwards Deming

The day I finally understood systems thinking, I was watching three teams celebrate victories that were killing our project.

It was 2015 at VF Corporation's innovation lab. We were building the smart jacket I described earlier. The materials science team had just solved a major problem: they'd optimized our fabric for flexibility. The electronics team was thrilled: they'd achieved the sensor accuracy we were looking for. The software team had cracked the algorithm for thermal comfort.

Three wins. Three celebrations. And I was staring at a project that was about to fail.

The fabric's flexibility conducted heat away from the sensors. The sensor accuracy required sampling rates that drained the battery in ninety minutes. The comfort algorithm demanded heating patterns the garment couldn't physically produce. Each team had optimized their piece. Each optimization broke something downstream.

That's when I saw it: Every department was winning. The project was losing.

The jacket only worked when we stopped thinking in departments. We had to see the whole system, how each decision rippled across every discipline. Optimize one node without seeing the others, and you broke something you couldn't even see.

That lesson has followed me through every technology shift since. The leaders who succeed aren't the ones with the deepest technical expertise. They're the ones who can see across boundaries, who understand that value flows through systems, not departments.

This chapter is about developing that sight.

## Why Departmental Thinking Traps Even Smart Leaders

Most leaders think in departments. They inherited org charts that slice businesses into functions: Marketing, Sales, Operations, Finance, HR. They measure success by departmental metrics. They're rewarded for departmental performance.

This makes sense historically. Departments create expertise, accountability, and manageable spans of control. The functional model powered the industrial economy for a century.

But departmental thinking has a fatal flaw: it improves parts at the expense of the whole.

When Marketing chases leads, they may attract customers who never convert, or worse, customers who convert but leave quickly. When Sales chases close rates, they may overpromise and create impossible expectations for Operations. When Operations chases efficiency, they may cut seemingly "unnecessary" elements that hurt the customer experience.

Each function wins. The business loses.

The real damage happens at the seams: the handoffs between departments where value leaks. Marketing hands leads to Sales. Sales hands deals to Operations. Operations hands experiences to Support. At every handoff, information is lost. Context vanishes. Accountability blurs.

Research shows that most customer complaints don't start within departments. They start at handoffs. The customer wasn't failed by Marketing or Sales or Operations. They were failed by the gap between them. [CITATION NEEDED]

Yet when leaders diagnose problems, they look inside departments. "We need better marketing." "We need stronger sales training." "We need operations improvements." They rarely examine the handoffs.

Departmental thinking is a trap. And AI makes it worse.

## Why AI Makes Broken Systems Fail Faster

AI is a force multiplier. That's exactly what makes it dangerous for organizations with the wrong mental model.

If your marketing team adopts AI to generate more leads, they'll generate more leads faster. If those leads are the wrong leads (customers who don't fit, who leave quickly, who drain support), you'll create more of the wrong customers, faster.

If your sales team uses AI to close more deals, they'll close more deals faster. If those deals are overpromised, if they set expectations Operations can't meet, you'll create more disappointed customers, faster.

If you just plug AI into your current processes without redesigning them, all you'll get is *faster bureaucracy*. You'll generate bad reports at the speed of light.

AI amplifies whatever pattern already exists. In organizations with strong systems, AI speeds up value creation. In organizations with weak handoffs and misaligned incentives, AI speeds up breakdown.

**The software is soft. The organization is hard.**

We focus on the technology because it's visible and exciting. But the real barrier to AI value isn't the AI; it's the organizational structures, incentive systems, and processes that the AI must work within. You can change software in days. Changing how people work together can take years.

Research from 2025 confirms this gap. According to research from the Oliver Wyman Forum, only about 6% of firms are seeing real bottom-line impact from their AI investments. The rest remain trapped in disconnected pilots and tactical experiments. The difference between leaders and laggards isn't the sophistication of their AI models. It's the clarity of their underlying operating model. [CITATION NEEDED: Oliver Wyman Forum report title, date, and URL]

I've watched companies adopt AI with huge enthusiasm and emerge worse than before. Not because the technology failed. Because they applied powerful acceleration to a broken model.

### The Productivity J-Curve

In 1890, factories ran on steam. A single engine in the basement powered a central shaft that turned belts connected to every machine. The physics of the power source dictated the workflow: you stacked the factory vertically to stay close to the shaft, and all machines ran together or not at all.

When electricity arrived, factory owners rushed to adopt it. They ripped out their steam engines, installed electric motors in the basement, hooked them to the same central shaft, and waited for productivity to soar.

They waited almost 30 years. Productivity barely moved.

The owners had made one fundamental error: they swapped the power source but kept the central shaft. They plugged the future into the architecture of the past. Only when someone realized you could put small motors on individual machines, redesign the layout by workflow, and eliminate the shaft entirely did the gains appear. They had to redesign the organization, not just the engine.

Research from the National Bureau of Economic Research shows this pattern repeats with every major technology shift: organizations often see productivity fall after investing in new technology. Economists call this the "productivity J-curve."[^jcurve] The decline isn't failure; it's the cost of co-invention, the development of new processes, structures, and skills required to use the technology well.[^complementarities]

Many organizations adopting AI today are electrifying the steam engine: applying new technology to old structures and wondering why the promised gains don't appear. Small teams can move through the J-curve faster, with less legacy to untangle and more freedom to experiment. But getting there requires clarity about your system before you apply acceleration.

The first step to AI readiness isn't adopting AI. It's seeing your business clearly.

## A Better Lens: Your Business as a Single Machine

What if instead of seeing your business as a collection of departments, you saw it as a single machine: a closed-loop engine that converts customer need into delivered value, loyalty, and profit?

This is the Value Flow Engine model. It becomes the foundation for AI-ready leadership.

**Key Insight:** Your business is not functions and departments. It is a closed-loop value engine. Your job as a leader is to strengthen flow, remove friction, reduce leakage, and increase surplus.

The Value Flow Engine has three layers working together:

Layer A: Operational Flow, the core sequence from customer need to loyal customer

Layer B: Reputation Flywheel, the amplification loop where loyalty creates pull

Layer C: Financial Layer, the economics that determine sustainability and growth

[GRAPHIC: framework_ch01_value-flow-engine-overview_v1.png]

Caption: The Value Flow Engine, three interconnected layers of business value creation

Let me walk you through each layer.

## Layer A: The Operational Flow

The operational flow is the core of your business. It's the sequence that converts unmet customer need into delivered value and, ultimately, loyalty.

The flow has six nodes:

### Node 1: Customer Need

Every business begins here. A customer has an unmet need: a problem to solve, a job to complete, a desire to fulfill. This need carries emotional stakes (fear, urgency, hope) and economic stakes (lost time, money, risk).

Without genuine need, nothing else matters. Many businesses fail not because they can't deliver value, but because they're solving problems customers don't actually have.

Failure mode: Building capabilities without verified demand.

### Node 2: Visibility and Pull Signals

How do customers with genuine need discover, evaluate, and choose you? This is visibility: the ways the right customers recognize that you can solve their problem.

Visibility isn't just marketing. It's trust, relevance, timing, and clarity. It's the answer to: "When customers have this need, do they think of us? Do they trust us? Can they find us?"

Failure modes: Invisible to the market. Loud but untrusted. Visible to the wrong customers.

### Node 3: Value Creation System

This is the engine room: the workflows, people, tools, and decisions that transform inputs into outcomes. It's where your organization actually creates value.

The core cycle is: Discover → Design → Build → Activate.

This node determines quality, cost to deliver, scalability, and consistency. It's where most AI investment lands, but applying AI here without understanding the full engine leads to local improvements and missed opportunities.

Failure modes: High cost, low consistency, quality variance, bottlenecks.

### Node 4: Delivered Value

What tangible outcomes does the customer actually receive? This is the moment of truth, where operational reality meets customer perception.

Delivered value isn't just the product or service. It's the complete experience: was it on time, was it as promised, was the interaction pleasant, did it actually solve the problem?

Failure modes: Outputs without outcomes. Unclear success criteria. Experience that undermines the product.

### Node 5: Retention Loop

Does the customer come back? Ongoing engagement, repeat purchases, renewals, expansions: the retention loop is where one-time value becomes relationship value.

Critically, the retention loop feeds back into the Value Creation System. Returning customers inform improvements. They provide data that makes the next delivery better.

Failure mode: No feedback mechanism. Customers treated as one-time transactions.

### Node 6: Loyal Customer

The customer now prefers you, trusts you, and speaks for you. They produce reviews, testimonials, referrals, and case studies.

Loyal customers are an asset, not just a metric. They reduce customer acquisition cost. They increase lifetime value. And critically, they create something the next layer depends on: proof.

Failure mode: Satisfied customers who never become advocates.

## Layer B: The Reputation Flywheel

Here's where the engine becomes powerful.

Loyal customers produce social proof: reviews, testimonials, word of mouth, case studies. This proof creates pull signals that amplify visibility. New customers discover you not because you shouted louder, but because satisfied customers spoke for you.

Loyalty → Social Proof → Pull Signals → New Visibility → New Demand

This is the reputation flywheel. When it's spinning, customer acquisition becomes easier and cheaper. Your marketing budget magnifies impact because it's amplified by customer advocacy. Your brand becomes an asset that compounds.

When the flywheel stalls, you're stuck in an expensive grind: paying for every customer, fighting for attention, competing on price.

Most businesses focus on Layer A (operational flow) and undervalue Layer B (reputation flywheel). They work hard to deliver value but never capture the proof that would make the next sale easier.

**Key Question:** Is your business creating loyal customers who create pull? Or are you trapped in a cycle of paid acquisition without organic amplification?

## Layer C: The Financial Layer

Below the operational and reputation layers runs the economic reality:

Value Captured − Cost to Deliver = Value Surplus

This is the equation that determines whether your engine is sustainable.

Value Captured is what customers pay for the outcomes you deliver.

Cost to Deliver is everything required to create and deliver that value.

Value Surplus is what remains: the margin that funds reinvestment, resilience, innovation, talent, and growth.

Healthy engines create surplus that gets reinvested into improving the engine. Better tools, better talent, better processes. This reinvestment increases quality and decreases cost, which creates more surplus, which enables more reinvestment.

This is the compounding mechanism. It's what separates businesses that get stronger over time from businesses that slowly fade.

## Using the Lens: Where Is Value Leaking?

Once you see your business as a Value Flow Engine, you ask different questions.

Instead of "Which department needs improvement?", you ask: "Where is value leaking in my engine?"

Leakage happens at predictable points:

Leakage at Need: Building for customers who don't exist, or for problems they don't prioritize.

Leakage at Visibility: The right customers have the problem but never find you, or find you but don't trust you.

Leakage at Value Creation: High cost, low consistency, quality problems that hurt delivered value.

Leakage at Delivery: Value is created but not perceived. The customer received the output but didn't recognize the outcome.

Leakage at Retention: Customers are satisfied but not retained. No feedback loop. No relationship development.

Leakage at Loyalty: Satisfied customers never become advocates. The reputation flywheel never spins.

Leakage at Financials: Value is captured but cost to deliver consumes the surplus. No reinvestment. No compounding.

I see this pattern constantly. A CEO I've advised, we'll call her Sarah, runs a B2B software company. Her numbers looked strong on paper. Marketing had doubled lead volume with targeted campaigns. Sales was closing 40% more deals than the previous year. Implementation had cut onboarding time from twelve weeks to six.

Yet 43% of new customers were churning within the first year. Growth had stalled. Margins were shrinking. And the company's reputation was sliding.

When Sarah applied the Value Flow Engine lens, she discovered something her departmental metrics had hidden.

The problem was the Marketing-to-Sales handoff. Marketing was generating leads from companies that matched the demographic profile (size, industry, revenue) but not the readiness profile. Many leads came from companies with legacy systems that couldn't integrate with Sarah's platform, or from buyers who lacked internal IT support for implementation.

Sales was closing those customers anyway, overpromising capabilities to hit quota. Implementation was delivering on time, but into environments where the product couldn't succeed. Customers blamed the software. The reputation flywheel was spinning backward.

The fix wasn't in any single department. Sarah added a "technical fit assessment" at the handoff between Marketing and Sales. Leads that failed the assessment were routed to a nurture track rather than to quota-carrying reps. Sales compensation shifted from pure close rates to a blend that included 90-day retention.

Within two quarters, close rates dropped by 15%, but churn dropped by 28%. Net revenue actually increased. Reviews improved. The flywheel started spinning forward.

She didn't need new departments. She needed a new lens.

Here's what Sarah understood that many leaders miss: if she had applied AI to the old process, she would have accelerated the breakdown. AI-powered lead scoring would have generated more bad-fit leads faster. AI-assisted sales would have closed more doomed deals at scale. The engine was broken; AI would have been a force multiplier on dysfunction. Only after fixing the handoff, after making the engine healthy, could she ask where AI might amplify the gains. The Value Flow Engine lens didn't just diagnose the problem. It revealed which patterns were safe to accelerate.

## Where AI Fits in the Engine

Now you can see where AI belongs.

AI is not the engine. AI is a force multiplier inside the engine.

For Sarah, the Marketing-to-Sales handoff was the obvious starting point: AI could score leads for technical readiness, predict integration complexity, flag implementation risks before a rep ever made a call. But every node in the engine has similar potential:

At Need: AI can analyze market signals, find unmet needs, predict demand.

At Visibility: AI can personalize outreach, improve messaging, create content at scale.

At Value Creation: AI can automate workflows, reduce cost, increase quality, speed up cycles.

At Delivery: AI can personalize experience, predict issues, close feedback loops.

At Retention: AI can predict churn, personalize engagement, spot expansion opportunities.

At Loyalty: AI can find advocates, ease referrals, capture and spread proof.

At Financials: AI can improve pricing, reduce cost, forecast accurately.

But here's the critical insight: AI amplifies the engine's existing patterns.

Apply AI to a broken engine, and you accelerate breakdown. Apply AI to a well-designed engine, and you accelerate compounding.

This is why mindset comes first. Before you ask "What can I automate?", you must ask "Is my engine healthy? Where is value leaking? What would I be amplifying?"

**The AI-Ready Leader's Question:** Not "What can I automate?" but "Where is value leaking in my engine, and what can AI amplify?"

## The Mindset Shift

Adopting the Value Flow Engine lens requires real mental rewiring.

Old Mindset:

"We need better marketing, sales, ops."

"Which department is underperforming?"

"Let's add AI to this function."

New Mindset:

"Where is value leaking in our engine?"

"What happens at the handoffs?"

"What would AI amplify: health or dysfunction?"

This shift has practical implications.

When you see the engine, you stop accepting departmental metrics as proof of success. Strong marketing metrics mean nothing if leads leak at the sales handoff. Strong sales metrics mean nothing if customers leak at retention.

When you see the engine, you invest in handoffs, not just functions. You create shared metrics that span departments. You design feedback loops that close the gaps.

When you see the engine, you approach AI strategically. You find the nodes where amplification would create the most value, and you address the nodes where amplification would cause the most damage.

## Practical Application

Here's how to apply the Value Flow Engine lens in practice:

Step 1: Map Your Current Engine

Draw the six nodes for your business. Where does need come from? How do customers find you? What are your value creation workflows? How is value delivered? What drives retention? What creates loyalty?

Step 2: Find Leakage Points

Walk the flow with honest eyes. Where is value leaking?

Demand that never becomes visible (acquisition problems)

Value created but not perceived (delivery problems)

Customers satisfied but not retained (relationship problems)

Loyalty that never converts to advocacy (proof capture problems)

Step 3: Examine the Handoffs

Look specifically at transitions between nodes. What information is lost? What context vanishes? Where does accountability blur?

Step 4: Assess the Financial Layer

The formula is simple: Value Captured − Cost to Deliver = Value Surplus. The calculation rarely is. What matters is the trend. If surplus is shrinking, your engine has a leak. The earlier nodes will tell you where.

Step 5: Ask the AI Question

For each node, ask: "If we applied AI here, what would we amplify?" Be honest. If the answer is "dysfunction," fix the node before adding acceleration.

## Chapter Summary

Departmental thinking creates local wins while value leaks at handoffs

Your business is a Value Flow Engine: a closed-loop system that converts need into value, loyalty, and surplus

The engine has three layers: Operational Flow, Reputation Flywheel, and Financial Layer

The leader's job is to strengthen flow, remove friction, reduce leakage, and increase surplus

The productivity J-curve explains why AI often makes things worse before better: co-invention costs must be paid before gains appear

Small teams can move through the J-curve faster than large organizations

AI amplifies whatever pattern exists, whether dysfunction or health

Before asking "What can I automate?", ask "Where is value leaking?"

**Key Takeaway:** The first step to AI readiness isn't adopting AI. It's seeing your business as a system. Once you see the engine, you can lead with clarity instead of confusion.

Value Flow Engine: Quick Reference

Three Layers: Operational Flow → Reputation Flywheel → Financial Layer

Six Nodes: Need → Visibility → Value Creation → Delivered Value → Retention → Loyalty

Diagnostic Question: Where is value leaking in your engine?

Signature Phrase: "Fix leaks first; amplify second."

Now that you can see the engine, a natural question arises: How do you build your own ability to use AI within it? That's where we turn next: to the progression from understanding AI to applying it to leading others through it. Welcome to the 3L Ladder.

**Start Today:**

1. Draw your business as a Value Flow Engine: map the six nodes and find which handoffs feel "leaky"

2. Ask your team: "If everyone is winning, why might the business still be losing?"

3. Pick one node and investigate: What would AI amplify there, health or dysfunction?

[^jcurve]: Erik Brynjolfsson, Daniel Rock, and Chad Syverson, "The Productivity J-Curve: How Intangibles Complement General Purpose Technologies," NBER Working Paper No. 25148 (October 2018).

[^complementarities]: Timothy F. Bresnahan and Manuel Trajtenberg, "General Purpose Technologies: 'Engines of Growth'?" Journal of Econometrics 65, no. 1 (1995): 83-108.
